# DA -- Environment -- DGHL

This file is divided into sections depending on the aff you are
debating. Some assembly is required depending on the aff you are
debating. Pick a link, pick an impact, and know where stuff is. It is
meant to cover various mineral/resource extraction affs. Uniqueness
arguments are usually embedded into the link or addressed in the
internal link evidence. The best way to find stuff is just to read the
whole file and reorganize it yourself because there is a lot of overlap
across the sections.

Thanks to Justin Chang, Austin Yoo, Gerald Okuna, Vincent Zhu, Dax
Weitekamper, Aalekh Arapalli, and Hanbo Song for their hard work!

## Uniqueness

### U\-\--Drilling\-\--2NC

#### No one wants to drill in the Arctic.

Scott L. **Montgomery 25**, Lecturer at the University of Washington
Jackson School of International Studies, M.S. in Geological and Earth
Sciences from Cornell University, "White House plans for Alaskan oil and
gas face some hurdles -- including from Trump and the petroleum
industry," The Conversation, 04-11-2025, accessed 07-05-2025,
https://theconversation.com/white-house-plans-for-alaskan-oil-and-gas-face-some-hurdles-including-from-trump-and-the-petroleum-industry-254040,
DG

The second [Trump]{.underline} administration has [launched the next
stage in the]{.underline} half-century-long [battle between commerce and
conservation over Alaskan oil and gas development]{.underline}. But [its
moves are delivering a **mixed message** to the petroleum
industry]{.underline}.

[The administration has]{.underline} opened -- or [reopened]{.underline}
-- [large swaths of government land in Alaska to oil and gas
drilling]{.underline}, though [only some]{.underline} of those
opportunities [have drawn]{.underline} much [commercial
interest]{.underline} in recent years. And an 800-mile pipeline across
Alaska that the administration says it supports is not yet funded, and
other administration policies risk turning off prospective partners.

President Donald Trump says he wants to grow oil and gas production and
advance the goal of what he calls U.S. "energy dominance." The White
House says that term means both reducing the amount of energy imported
from other countries and increasing the amount of energy exported from
the U.S., especially to allies.

The U.S. is already the world's largest producer and exporter of natural
gas as well as the largest producer of crude oil. And the nation's oil
industry boomed under the Biden administration. However, the U.S. does
import an average of over 6 million barrels per day of crude oil, most
of it from Canada.

[Trump's efforts seek to boost U.S. production]{.underline} to still
greater heights [by expanding access to areas for drilling
and]{.underline} building [related infrastructure]{.underline}. But as a
former petroleum geoscientist and industry observer, I would suggest
[his various actions]{.underline}, taken as a whole, [may have **more
limited effects than he seems to hope**]{.underline}.

Returned to leasing

In one of his first executive orders after retaking office on Jan. 20,
2025, Trump declared that the U.S. would develop Alaska's petroleum
resources "to the fullest extent possible."

The Biden administration had banned oil leasing in three areas of
Alaska. One was all but 400,000 acres in the coastal plain portion of
the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge. Another was a 13-million-acre swath
of the National Petroleum Reserve-Alaska, a massive parcel of federal
land west of the refuge. The third area was 44 million acres of the
offshore coastal portion of the northern Bering Sea, based on concerns
for tribal rights and the migration routes of marine mammals.

Trump moved quickly to reverse all these bans, describing them as an
"assault on Alaska's sovereignty and its ability to responsibly develop
(its) resources for the benefit of the Nation." And Trump went farther,
expanding the available land by an additional 6 million acres in the
petroleum reserve and another 1.1 million acres of the wildlife refuge.

All those [areas are home to many different types of wildlife, as well
as Indigenous groups]{.underline}.

The view of industry

[For the petroleum industry, I expect these actions are **both welcome
and irrelevant**. Reopening the]{.underline} northeastern portion of the
[petroleum reserve creates a real opportunity]{.underline}: Exploration
has found a significant amount of oil and gas in that area, and
indications are that there may be more yet to discover.

[But prospects on the land in the wildlife refuge and the shallow waters
of the Bering Sea are **not likely of much interest to drilling
companies**]{.underline} unless oil prices rise significantly from their
levels in early 2025. [There is no established production in either
area]{.underline} at present. And, though the refuge has oil and gas
potential, [there are **no roads or pipelines**]{.underline}, and
[Arctic drilling is **especially expensive**]{.underline}.

In fact, [the last two attempts by the government to lease oil
development rights in the wildlife refuge drew **very little
interest**]{.underline}. In 2020, the first Trump administration teamed
with Republicans in Congress to overcome long-standing legal and
political opposition to leasing in the refuge. But the 2021 lease sale
was a bust, with none of the top oil producers in the state
participating.

[A second round of bidding, in January 2025, **received no interest at
all from oil companies**]{.underline}.

#### 'Drill baby drill' is all hype.

accessed 7/5/2025) //daxw

Analysts said [drillers]{.underline} may [not]{.underline} be [in a
hurry to take advantage of Trump\'s order]{.underline} on Monday [titled
\"Unleashing Alaska's extraordinary resource potential]{.underline}.\",
opens new tab

The [executive order would reopen vast areas for drilling and
mining]{.underline}, and expedite permits for projects, part of Trump\'s
sweeping plan to maximize oil and gas production while reversing former
President Joe Biden\'s policies encouraging the transition to renewable
energy sources to fight climate change.

\"Many of these areas have been closed for a good long while,\" said
Dustin Meyers, senior vice president of policy at the American Petroleum
Institute, a trade organization that represents major oil firms
including Exxon Mobil, Chevron Corp, and ConocoPhillips.

\"[There is always the risk that these areas could be reclosed after the
next election cycle]{.underline},\" he said, adding that is the [key
issue that could temper interest from oil companies in the short term to
pursue new drilling projects there]{.underline}.

[Drilling in the Arctic and Alaska is a high-risk endeavor]{.underline},
involving [decades of work and billions of dollars of
investment]{.underline}.

Exxon (XOM.N), Chevron (CVX.N), Conoco (COP.N), and Occidental Petroleum
(OXY.N), did not comment.

Conoco is among the most active oil companies in Alaska's Arctic, and
secured a federal approval from the Biden administration for its \$8
billion Willow project there in 2023, angering environmental groups.

The U.S. drilling industry trade group AXPC also did not comment.

A [source at one major U.S. oil company]{.underline}, who asked not to
be named discussing the matter, [said many companies are unlikely to
pursue projects in Alaska based on Trump\'s executive order alone,
seeking long-term certainty like an act of Congress]{.underline}.

Energy consultancy Rystad said Trump's use of the \"[drill, baby,
drill]{.underline}\" mantra in his inauguration speech [overestimates
the industry\'s willingness to prioritize growth over generating
shareholder returns.]{.underline}

[Drilling in Alaska\'s pristine Arctic refuge has long been a source of
friction between Alaska lawmakers and tribal corporations seeking to
open more acres to drilling to spur economic growth, and Democratic
presidential administrations that sought to preserve the local ecosystem
and wildlife.]{.underline}

A 2017 tax law during [Trump]{.underline}\'s first term mandated oil and
gas lease sales in the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge, a 19
million-acre sanctuary for species including polar bears and Porcupine
caribou. [But interest has been sparse.]{.underline}

The Biden Interior Department received [no bids from energy companies
last year when it offered 400,000 acres of the refuge]{.underline}, the
minimum amount required.

[ANWR]{.underline}'s wild landscape lacks roads and public facilities,
but its 1.6 million-acre coastal area along the Beaufort Sea [is
estimated to have up to 11.8 billion barrels of recoverable oil, enough
to supply the U.S. for more than a year and a half]{.underline} at
current rates.

Before leaving office this month, Biden banned new offshore oil and gas
drilling in federal waters off the East and West coasts, the eastern
Gulf of Mexico and portions of the northern Bering Sea in Alaska. The
move was considered mostly symbolic since it mainly covered zones that
have no important prospects.

[No oil or gas company sought to drill in a contentious wildlife refuge
in Alaska, the Interior Department announced]{.underline} Wednesday.

As required by a 2017 law, the [Biden administration offered the private
sector the chance to drill on tracts in the Arctic National Wildlife
Refuge]{.underline}.

[However]{.underline}, it said Wednesday that [no company submitted a
bid to do so]{.underline}. The deadline to submit bids to drill was
Monday.

"The [lack of interest from oil companies in development in the Arctic
National Wildlife Refuge reflects what we and they have known all along
--- there are some places too special and sacred to put at risk with oil
and gas drilling]{.underline}," Laura Daniel-Davis, acting deputy
secretary of the Interior Department, said in a written statement.

On the campaign trail, President-elect Trump said he wanted drilling in
the wildlife refuge, saying in August that he would "get it going very
quickly."

But if companies are unwilling to drill there, that would complicate his
efforts.

Whether to allow drilling in the refuge has been a source of partisan
conflict for many years.

#### It's [too expensive]{.underline} and [politically costly]{.underline}. 

Jennifer A. **Dlouhy 25**, Reporter at Bloomberg News, "Oil companies
decline to bid for Arctic Refuge drilling rights," World Oil,
01-08-2025, accessed 07-05-2025,

[Oil companies declined to bid]{.underline} in a U.S. government auction
[to drill in the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge, as **industry interest
wanes** in a region]{.underline} that's [rich with crude but **difficult
to develop**]{.underline}.

The [Interior]{.underline} Department said it [received no sealed
bids]{.underline} for the auction mandated by Congress before those
offers were set to be opened Friday. It marked [the second time in four
years an auction of oil and gas leases]{.underline} in the refuge's
1.6-million-acre coastal plain **[flopped]{.underline}**, coming after a
2021 sale held under President-elect Donald Trump drew just 11 high
bids, most of which were lodged by an Alaska economic development
corporation. Ultimately, [all]{.underline} of the [leases sold
in]{.underline} that [2021]{.underline} auction [were **forfeited**,
with two relinquished and seven others canceled]{.underline} by
Interior.

[The industry's no-show]{.underline} this week [underscores the **legal,
social and economic challenges of drilling in the Coastal Plain**,
despite U.S. Geological Survey estimates the region might hold between
4.3 billion and 11.8 billion barrels of]{.underline} technically
recoverable [crude]{.underline}.

"The [lack of interest]{.underline} from oil companies in development in
the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge [reflects what we and they have
known all along: There are some places **too special and sacred to put
at risk with oil and gas drilling**]{.underline}," said Laura
Daniel-Davis, the acting deputy Interior secretary.

The idea of tapping the 19-million acre refuge for its potential oil
bounty has tantalized Washington for decades. Oil industry allies and
advocates have long championed lease sales in ANWR as a way to boost
production on Alaska's North Slope and supply more crude to the Trans
Alaska Pipeline System that provides a vital conduit for crude.

Yet [companies]{.underline} that lay their eyes on the region [would
come up against more than just reputational risks.
Developing]{.underline} the area [would require they overcome **costly
logistical challenges, navigate intense permitting requirements** and
**deal with opposition from environmental groups**]{.underline},
analysts have warned.

[The **high costs** and **challenges of Arctic oil
development**]{.underline} --- most [vividly demonstrated by Shell Plc's
struggles to mount exploratory drilling in the Chukchi Sea]{.underline}
more than a decade ago --- [led energy companies to **forfeit drilling
rights** in U.S. Arctic waters north of Alaska too, despite the
potentially 27-billion-barrel bounty]{.underline}.

#### Even if opportunities exist, the industry [won't bite]{.underline}.

Heather **Richards 24**, Interior Department and National Park Service
Reporter at E&E News, "Trump gets second chance to drill ANWR. Will oil
companies bite?", E&E News, 12-04-2024, accessed 07-05-2025,
https://www.eenews.net/articles/trump-gets-second-chance-to-drill-anwr-will-oil-companies-bite,
DG

[Regardless of the **political interest** in development in
ANWR]{.underline}, which is long-standing for Alaska leaders and many
North Slope Alaska Natives, [the oil and gas industry will ultimately be
the test of Trump's]{.underline} ANWR [policies]{.underline}.

[The North Slope is far from Asian and Lower 48 markets]{.underline} to
sell its oil and gas. [The refuge]{.underline} also [has **no current
infrastructure** like pipelines to move oil and gas. Major oil
companies]{.underline}, like BP and Shell, [have **exited Alaska** and
reduced the number of well-funded major companies likely to bid in
federal lease sales]{.underline}.

Meanwhile, some [major insurers and banks have **increasingly expressed
reservations** about supporting drilling and mining in the Arctic
because of its cultural and biological sensitivity and vulnerability to
climate change]{.underline}. The Arctic is warming at twice the rate of
the rest of the globe, according to NOAA.

The only certain bidder appears to be the Alaska Industrial Development
and Export Authority, which in October approved a plan to use up to \$20
million to bid in the upcoming ANWR auction. The authority was the
majority bidder in the Trump-era sale in 2021.

Lavin said [the difficult oil market is **one of the strongest
protections against**]{.underline} the Trump administration's
**[increasing drilling access]{.underline}**.

"[The **headwinds are substantial**, even if drilling the Arctic refuge
is what you want]{.underline} to do," he said.

The former Trump Interior official who spoke to E&E News said geological
data --- obtained through seismic surveys to show what's beneath the
refuge's surface --- could galvanize industry interest.

Seismic technology uses sound echoes to digitally create a 3D rendering
of the subsurface, helping companies identify where oil and gas deposits
are located and strategize how to tap them. The U.S. Geological Survey
estimates as much as 10 billion barrels of oil under the refuge's
surface. But that estimate hasn't been fortified with modern testing
techniques.

The last attempt at shooting seismic in the refuge came from an Alaska
Native organization --- the Kaktovik Iñupiat Corp. (KIC) --- in 2021.
KIC is one of the only entities to ever have drilled a well in the
refuge. KIC, with partners BP, Chevron and Arctic Slope Regional,
drilled a test well in ANWR in 1985. But data from the well has not been
made public.

The Biden administration voided KIC's application to shoot seismic in
2021 for failing to complete required overhead surveys to look for polar
bears.

KIC disputed Interior's arguments, saying the Biden administration
intentionally stalled the application. KIC and the Alaska Oil and Gas
Association did not respond to a request for comment for this story.

Seismic testing would be limited to leased acres in the refuge, under
the Biden administration's final supplemental environmental review for
the ANWR oil program. Trump is expected to lift that restriction, but
[Interior officials]{.underline} could still [face hurdles]{.underline}.

[Seismic testing uses heavy trucks that can crush polar bear dens, a
protected species]{.underline}. The bears are classified as threatened
by the Fish and Wildlife Service, and a subpopulation of polar bear
females often spend winter in snow dens with their cubs in the refuge's
coastal plain. They are protected by the Marine Mammal Protection Act.

[The winter months are]{.underline} also [when oil and gas activity is
allowed]{.underline} in the Arctic because the tundra is frozen and less
vulnerable to traffic damage. The [timing makes a seismic program
challenging]{.underline}.

Even if the Trump administration is supportive of seismic, [it will be
difficult for federal agencies in Alaska to approve a seismic program
because of the high risk to bears]{.underline}, said Lavin with
Defenders of Wildlife.

From seismic impacts to climate, an oil program doesn't fit in the
refuge, he said.

"**[It just kind of sticks out like a sore thumb as a really bad
idea]{.underline}**," Lavin said.

### U\-\--Pipelines\-\--2NC

#### Alaska LNG is a joke\-\--new investments are [non-binding]{.underline}, [far off]{.underline}, and likely to get cancelled.

Alex **DeMarban 25**, Reporter at Anchorage Daily News, "Alaska LNG has
caught a wave of high-level attention. Is it winning over its
skeptics?", Anchorage Daily News, 06-05-2025, accessed 07-05-2025,
https://www.adn.com/business-economy/energy/2025/06/05/alaska-lng-has-caught-a-wave-of-high-level-attention-is-it-winning-over-its-skeptics,
DG

[A rare visit to Alaska]{.underline} this week [by high-level Asian
dignitaries and top members of the Trump administration]{.underline} has
[brought new attention to]{.underline} the \$44 billion [Alaska
LNG]{.underline} project.

But [state lawmakers and industry observers]{.underline} who have been
[skeptical of the project say **huge questions remain** about a
mega-project that Alaska businesses and leaders]{.underline}, in one
form or another, [have **tried and failed to build for
generations**]{.underline}.

The Alaska LNG project would deliver vast amounts of natural gas from
the North Slope in an 800-mile pipeline, super-chill it in Southcentral
Alaska, and transport the liquefied natural gas overseas to countries
like Japan, Korea and Taiwan. It would be built in phases, with an \$11
billion portion for the gas line providing natural gas in Alaska in a
first phase, followed by construction of other portions such as the
liquefaction plant and marine terminal to support Asian exports.

Alaska LNG is the latest project to propose tapping North Slope gas.
Similar Alaska gas line projects have failed for half a century, often
the victim of high price tags and a lack of investment, generating
skepticism among many Alaskans that the project will ever be built.

Delegations from those Asian countries and others came to Alaska this
week, expressing interest in the project and also seeking to learn more
about it. A Taiwanese official even said Alaska LNG could one day become
that country's primary source of energy, if the project is built.

They were joined by three top officials with the administration of
President Donald Trump who vowed full support for the project, during a
rare trip to the Alaska oil and gas fields near the Arctic Ocean where
the project would originate.

[Trump]{.underline} has [made Alaska LNG one of his pet projects,
calling it]{.underline} "[truly spectacular]{.underline}" in his joint
speech to Congress in March. He's urged Asian countries to invest in the
project or buy its gas to reduce their trade deficits with the U.S.

The project also received a lot of focus at Gov. Mike Dunleavy's
three-day sustainable energy conference in Anchorage, including on
Thursday when the head of Glenfarne Group, the private company pursuing
the project, said first gas could reach Alaskans in 2028 --- though he
acknowledged it won't be easy.

Lawmakers and industry observers said the spotlight on the project this
week will help potential investors better understand its virtues, such
as:

• Its proximity to Asian countries that want secure energy sources.

• Its existing federal permitting approvals and a ready right-of-way
along the existing trans-Alaska pipeline.

• Its support from both the Trump and former Biden administrations.

[Alaska Senate Majority Leader Cathy Giessel said it's great that the
project has fresh interest, but she still has plenty of **doubts it will
be built**]{.underline}.

One concern is that the \$44 billion cost estimate is likely too low,
she said.

"I'm remaining pretty skeptical," Giessel said in an interview Thursday.

She'll remain so, **[until a company puts up]{.underline}** maybe \$10
billion or \$**[20 billion to build it]{.underline}**, she said.

The project has a lot of positives, no doubt, she said.

"At the same time, [the **cost of materials** and the **workforce** is a
whole other calculation]{.underline}, and I think [they're way
underestimating that in their optimistic pursuit of this]{.underline},"
she said.

Rep. Chuck Kopp, R-Anchorage, said the interest from potential Asian
partners this week and other positive developments for the project give
him confidence that it will be built starting in the next two or three
years.

"We've seen major players come and go and commitments signed and
scrapped, so I get why folks are weary," he said. "But you know, here's
the question, in this moment when Alaska LNG has serious investment
interest, federal support and desperate in-state energy need, are we
going to let old disappointments shut the door on a real opportunity?
I'm not.\"

\$115B in 'expressed interest' raises questions

[Glenfarne said]{.underline} in a statement this week that [more than 50
companies from around the world have expressed interest]{.underline} for
"over \$115 billion of contract value for various partnerships with the
project."

The partnerships would include "equipment and material supply, services,
investment, and customer agreements," according to Glenfarne's
statement.

**[Who are the companies?]{.underline}**

Glenfarne spokesperson Micah Hirschfield declined to disclose that
information Tuesday.

Glenfarne early in the week planned [a media briefing]{.underline} on
Thursday where questions could be posed to Brendan Duval, Glenfarne's
chief executive.

But it [was canceled]{.underline} due to the company's busy agenda,
Hirschfield said Thursday morning.

Duval could not be reached for comment Thursday, Hirschfield said.

"Not going to be able to make it happen today," Hirschfield said in a
text. "Apologies."

Giessel said the announcement leaves many questions.

"OK, so in plain English, what does that mean?" she said. "**[Are they
ponying up money? Are they just saying they're interested in
participating in the project?]{.underline}**"

She said the project, which involves Alaska as a minority partner
alongside Glenfarne, should be much more public given its large size and
use of Alaska's resources.

Kopp said he's confident the announcement will soon be followed by
binding agreements, given that Glenfarne says more than 50 companies are
expressing interest.

"I don't think it's an exaggeration to think it's just months, within
two to three short months," he said.

Brad Keithley, a former oil and gas attorney and longtime industry
observer, said the [expression of interest is similar to
**nonbinding**]{.underline} "**[memorandums of
understanding]{.underline}**" that [the project has signed with foreign
entities **in the past, for potential partnerships or gas supply
agreements, that have amounted to nothing**]{.underline}.

"[They're trying a **shock-and-awe** with coming out with a big number
like that, but **this is no more than expression of
interest**]{.underline}," Keithley said. "**[We've seen lot of those
over the course of Alaska LNG history that never turn into solid
numbers]{.underline}**."

[They're making the announcement to get attention]{.underline} for the
project [in hopes that people get on board]{.underline}, Keithley said.
It's not surprising that **[Glenfarne]{.underline}** "**[dumped a number
out there and walked away from it]{.underline}**," he said.

Keithley said this week's events have brought renewed attention to the
pipeline, a plus. But ultimately, the economics of the project will
determine whether any entities will invest.

Sen. Jesse Bjorkman, R-Nikiski, said there's a lot of "momentum and
tailwind" behind the project, and that's exciting.

But he said there are plenty of "**[details and holes]{.underline}**
that **[need to be fleshed out]{.underline}**." His constituents have
concerns that ratepayers in Alaska could face high costs for the gas,
since the state's small population and businesses will be the buyers
before exports begin, following the \$11 billion construction of the
first phase.

"I know what matters to the people on the Kenai Peninsula is they want
their lights and their homes to be on and heated reliably, and they want
that to happen at the lowest cost possible," Bjorkman said.

Alaska House Speaker Bryce Edgmon said outside the conference at the
Dena'ina Civic and Convention Center that the attention on the project
has helped create buzz around it.

"The optimism meter has to be surging, there's no question about it," he
said.

"But the devil's in the details with this project, as they say," he
said.

[One question is the level of involvement of potential investors in the
project, if any]{.underline}, he said.

#### No pipeline expansion\-\--tariffs and lack of funding stop companies from taking advantage of the opportunity.

Scott L. **Montgomery 25**, Lecturer at the University of Washington
Jackson School of International Studies, M.S. in Geological and Earth
Sciences from Cornell University, "White House plans for Alaskan oil and
gas face some hurdles -- including from Trump and the petroleum
industry," The Conversation, 04-11-2025, accessed 07-05-2025,
https://theconversation.com/white-house-plans-for-alaskan-oil-and-gas-face-some-hurdles-including-from-trump-and-the-petroleum-industry-254040,
DG

[A strong gain for the petroleum industry would be a major new pipeline
to carry natural gas]{.underline} more than 800 miles south [from the
Prudhoe Bay area on the Arctic coast to a port near
Anchorage]{.underline} on south-central Alaska's Cook Inlet.

The idea has its own decades-long history, and has been both pushed
forward and set back over the years by changing economics, government
plans, and tribal interest and opposition.

The main challenge is that [there is **no way to transport natural gas**
off the North Slope]{.underline}. Since drilling began in the late
1970s, some has been used locally for heating and running equipment,
with the vast majority being reinjected into oil reservoir rock to help
maintain oil production.

Rising demand and elevated prices in Asia, however, suggest the project
could be profitable, despite the current cost estimate of US\$44
billion. Project plans indicate most of it would go to build a liquefied
natural gas export terminal near Anchorage, with the rest spent to
construct an 807-mile pipeline paralleling the existing Trans-Alaska
Pipeline, and a plant at Prudhoe Bay that would capture carbon from the
atmosphere, compress it and inject it into oil-producing reservoirs to
boost production.

The pipeline is designed to carry 3.3 billion cubic feet of natural gas
each day, which would make it one of the largest pipelines in North
America. The export terminal, to be built near the town of Nikiski on
Cook Inlet, would have a capacity of roughly 1 trillion cubic feet per
year, enough to heat about 15 million homes for a year.

The pipeline could take as little as two to three years to build, but
the terminal and carbon-capture plant would take longer -- five years or
so. The exports from Alaska could go to other ports in the U.S., but
they could also fetch higher prices in Japan, South Korea, Taiwan and
possibly China.

A wrench in the works

[Most of the permits needed for the pipeline-and-export-terminal project
have been secured]{.underline} by the Alaska Gasline Development
Corporation, a company created by the state of Alaska to build the
project.

[However, **no company or foreign government has**]{.underline} yet
**[agreed to foot the bill]{.underline}**, and [despite the support of
the Trump White House, there's **no indication the federal government
will do so** either]{.underline}.

The [Trump]{.underline} administration has also [created a new barrier
to the project]{.underline}. Its [sweeping tariffs and the resulting
trade war **crashed prices** in the global oil and gas
market]{.underline} in early April 2025.

In addition, [uncertainty about the permanence of tariffs or other
restrictions on]{.underline} international [trade are]{.underline} now
[**widespread** and **directly affect the oil industry**. Lower gas and
oil prices and less stability **make any project less
attractive**]{.underline}.

[It's true that Trump exempted oil and gas from his]{.underline} most
recent [tariffs. But **that matters less than the broader effect** the
trade war is already having]{.underline}, with analysts projecting it is
driving the global economy toward recession. [Less economic activity
means less demand for oil and gas, and]{.underline} therefore [less
incentive]{.underline} for companies [to]{.underline} drill new wells
and [build new pipelines]{.underline}.

To top everything off, [the White House slapped heavy tariffs on Japan,
South Korea and Taiwan, the very countries that might be inclined to
help fund the pipeline project]{.underline}. Even before the trade war,
they were hesitant about supporting it. The potential [suspension, or
reinstatement, or adjustment of tariffs is not likely to help them view
the situation as more stable]{.underline}.

[Those who favor oil and gas development in Alaska may be wondering
whether the president is truly on their side. It remains to be seen
whether their hopes might end up a casualty of White House economic
policy]{.underline}.

#### It has no investors.

Spencer **Kimball 25**, Energy Reporter at CNBC, "Trump administration
is convinced massive Alaska energy project will find investors despite
steep cost," CNBC, 06-02-2025, accessed 07-05-2025,
https://www.cnbc.com/2025/06/02/trump-administration-convinced-alaska-lng-will-find-investors-despite-cost.html,
DG

The Trump administration is confident that a massive liquified natural
gas project in Alaska will find investors despite its enormous cost.

President Donald [Trump]{.underline} has [pushed Alaska LNG as a
national priority]{.underline} since taking office. Alaska has already
spent years trying to build an 800-mile pipeline from the North Slope
above the Arctic Circle south to the Cook Inlet, where the gas would be
cooled and shipped to U.S. allies in Asia.

But [Alaska LNG has **never gotten off the ground** due to a
**stratospheric price tag** of more than \$40 billion.
Trump]{.underline} has [pushed Japan and South Korea]{.underline} in
particular [to invest]{.underline} in the project, [threatening them
with higher]{.underline} tariffs if they don't offer trade deals that
suit him.

"If you get the commercial offtakers for the gas, financing is pretty
straightforward," Energy Secretary Chris Wright told CNBC's Brian
Sullivan in Prudhoe Bay, Alaska. "There \[are\] countries around the
world looking to shrink their trade deficit with the United States, and
of course, a very easy way to do that is to buy more American energy,"
Wright said.

[Energy analysts]{.underline}, however, [are **skeptical**]{.underline}
of the project. [Alaska LNG]{.underline} "**[doesn't have a clear cut
commercial logic]{.underline}**," Alex Munton, director of global gas
and LNG research at Rapidan Energy, told CNBC in April.

"**[If it did, it would have had a lot more support]{.underline}** than
it has thus far, and [this project has been on the planning board for
**literally decades**]{.underline}," Munton said.

[The **sheer size of Alaska** and **proportion of intact
systems**]{.underline}, much of which is public land, [makes Alaska a
**singularly important** and **opportune place** for the **U**nited
**S**tates to consider natural climate solutions]{.underline} within its
own borders. Approximately [60%]{.underline} (900,000 km2 of 1.5 M km2)
[of Alaska's terrestrial surface area is federal land]{.underline}
(Figure 1). Nearly [2/3 of this is within an IUCN protected
category]{.underline} I-VI, [with management ranging from protections
for wildlife and recreational values]{.underline} \[e.g., Fish and

[development]{.underline} \[e.g., United States Forest Service (USFS),
