~~madness~~---and it's ~~maddening~~.

We all remember the 2010 BP blowout that killed 11 workers, dumped more
than 200 million gallons of toxic crude oil into the Gulf of Mexico, and
threw scores of thousands of people out of work. Watching oil gush into
pristine ocean waters for 87 days while the industry fumbled, unable to
stop it. The ghastly pictures of pelicans, dolphins, turtles and other
marine life dying and coated in oil that polluted more than 1,100 miles
of coast. And the presidential commission that told us what went wrong
and what we needed to do to prevent anything like it from happening
again.

Trump wants us to forget all that and let the oil and gas industry have
its way with our workers, waters, and wildlife. Far from protecting us
from the next BP-type disaster, he's increasing the odds of another one.

If Trump gets his way, we'll know less about the dangers of offshore
drilling, we'll have fewer tools to reduce those risks, and we'll have
more waters and coasts exposed to hazard and harm.

And for what?

The United States will produce more crude oil today---9.2 million
barrels---than at any other time since the early 1970s. When you add
natural gas liquids, which are refined much like crude oil, we're up to
12.9 million barrels a day, our highest level ever and up 90 percent
over just the past decade.

To those who think oil is a national security commodity, we now export
6.2 million barrels of crude oil and refined petroleum products each
day---up 343 percent since 2008. That's enough to meet a third of our
daily demand, but the industry is shipping it overseas.

This isn't about national security or some speechwriter's pipe dreams of
energy "dominance." It's about putting fossil fuel profits first---and
putting the rest of us at risk. Its reckless. It's reprehensible. It's
wrong.

[There may be **nothing more essential** to the natural systems that
support and sustain **all life on earth** than clean and healthy oceans.
Oil and gas production at sea puts all that at risk of blowouts,
explosions, disastrous spills, and the sonic blasts used in exploration
that can be dangerous, even lethal, to whales and other marine
life]{.underline}. Burning oil and gas creates carbon pollution that
drives climate change---warming our oceans, raising sea levels, and
threatening our coastal communities. And much of that carbon pollution
settles into our oceans, making our waters more acidic and wreaking
havoc on shellfish, coral reefs, and other foundational forms of life at
sea.

[Protecting]{.underline} the [waters that **sustain life on our planet**
begins with ending our reliance on fossil fuels as quickly as
possible,]{.underline} by investing in energy efficiency so we do more
with less waste; modernizing our transportation systems; and getting
more clean power from the wind and sun.

[As we shift away from]{.underline} the [dirty fuels]{.underline} of the
past toward cleaner, smarter ways to power our future, [we must **limit,
not expand**, the waters that are exposed to the hazards of offshore
drilling]{.underline}. And we must do everything we can to reduce the
risks of what is, by its nature, dangerous work, by strengthening
commonsense safeguards, not weakening them.

#### Oil spills cause [extinction]{.underline}.

Etuk Etiese **Akpan 22**, Clean Nigeria Associates, June 2022,
Environmental Consequences of Oil Spills on Marine Habitats and the
Mitigating Measures---The Niger Delta Perspective, Journal of Geoscience
and Environmental Protection, 10(6),
https://www.scirp.org/journal/paperinformation.aspx?paperid=118339

A number of researches have been carried out on hydrocarbon polluted
marine environment. The studies include Abii and Nwosu (2009) study on
the effects of oil spillage on soil and Aghalino (2000) on the negative
impact of **[oil activities]{.underline}** on the marine wild life,
soil, air, water and the ecosystem of communities.

[The ecological effects include]{.underline} brownish vegetation and
**[soil erosion]{.underline}**, dead and **[extinction]{.underline}**
[of marine wild life]{.underline}, [**diminishing resources** of the
natural ecosystem]{.underline}, [fertile land turned
**barren**]{.underline} [and adverse effect on]{.underline} the life,
health and economy of the **[people]{.underline}**. In Amadi and Ue Bari
(1992) study in the rainforest ecosystem in Nigeria, soil and
microbiological properties were evaluated 17 years after oil spillage to
assess the effects of oil and interrelationship between the hydrocarbon
utilizing and nitrifying microorganisms in the marine ecosystem. The
study showed that organic carbon, total nitrogen, carbon/nitrogen ratio,
available phosphate and exchangeable potassium were high at moderate and
high impacted zones. Also the distribution of aerobic petroleum
hydrocarbon utilizing fungi and bacteria showed a lesser condition at
the moderately impacted zone than at the highly impacted zones.

[The effect of crude **oil pollution**]{.underline} on marine
environment, soil fertility and the growth of plants and uptake of
nutrients were investigated by Agbogidi, Eruotor and Akparabi (2007) by
growing corn on a soil polluted by crude oil. The soil was analyzed for
organic carbon, total and available nitrogen, extractable phosphate, and
exchangeable potassium, calcium, iron and manganes after each cropping.
It was observed that [germination and **yields** were drastically
reduced]{.underline} as the level of pollution increased. At 4.2 percent
crude oil pollution level, the average reductions were 50 percent in
germination and 92 percent in yield. The amount of organic carbon, total
nitrogen, extractable phosphate, and exchangeable potassium, iron and
manganese increased in the soil with level of crude oil addition, while
extractable phosphate and exchangeable calcium were reduced. The poor
growth was attributed to suffocation of plants caused by exclusion of
air by oil and exhaustion of oxygen by increased microbial activity,
interference with plant-soil-water relationships and toxicity from
sulfides and excess manganese produced during the decomposition of the
hydrocarbons.

Wokocha, Emeodu and Ihenko (2011) examined the impact of crude oil
spillage on the ecosystem, soil properties and food production in
Ogba/Egbema/ Ndoni Area in Rivers State, Nigeria. The results showed
that the pH status of soil in heavily contaminated and moderately
contaminated zones varied from acidic (pH 4.0) to neutral (pH 6.0). The
chemical properties of soil indicated that percentage organic matter
increased from 1.34 to 2.62, available phosphorus decreased from 15 ppm
in control to between 7.34 and 5.42 in soil polluted with high level of
crude oil. The result was in line with Amadi and Ue Bari (1992),
and Ogboghodo, Osemwota, Iruaga and Chikor (2000).

Andrade, Cavelo, Vega and Marcet (2004) in an experiment on the effect
of prestige oil on marine salt marsh ecosystem soils in the coast of
Galicia (Northern Spain) revealed that **[oil pollution]{.underline}**
[altered both physical and chemical soil properties, lowered porosity,
and increased resistance to penetration and hydrophobicity]{.underline}.
The **[crude oil spillage]{.underline}** [affected th]{.underline}e
physical, chemical and biological [properties of soil and the **entire
marine ecosystem**, resulting in **low food production**]{.underline}
[by reducing the nutrients availability]{.underline} in the soils
through increased soil acidity and toxicity of crude oil fractions. The
experiment on the effect of poultry manure on maize planted on crude oil
polluted soils showed that [percentage growth rate in plant height and
**yield** decreased with increase in crude **oil
contamination**]{.underline} (Ogboghodo et al., 2004).

**[Crude oil spillage]{.underline}** [also suppresses seed
**germination**]{.underline}, [regeneration and restoration]{.underline}
and caused cellular and stomata abnormalities (Gill & Sandota,
1976). Ekundayo, Emede and Osayande (2001) confirmed that in crude oil
polluted soils, possibility of grain yield is significantly reduced by
95 percent compared with the control. In a study of agricultural land in
an oil producing area around Qua Iboe River in the Eastern Niger Delta
of Nigeria, the fouled loamy soil samples polluted by crude oil were
treated using chemical degreasers and detergents (Essien & John, 2010).
The result of the treatments showed a significant effect on soil
properties and crop growth parameters; however recovery level was
significantly higher than the level of degradation, except in
infiltration rate. Soil pH increased by 26% in fouled soil, attributed
to bacterial biodegradation of crude oil under the anaerobic conditions
present in the soil macro and micro-pores, and indicated the tendency of
crude oil spills to buffer acidic soil to neutral. Hydraulic
conductivity with 45% - 67% reduction from 82.24 cm/day in the control
soil to 39.6 cm/day in polluted soil confirmed the blockage of polluted
soils micropores by oil films. Crop growth, indicated by root
elongation, diminished to 7.4 ± 0.64 cm in polluted soil ecosystem
compared to13.47 ± 6.40 cm in the control soil ecosystem.

2.1. The Marine Habitats

A habitat is an ecological or environmental area inhabited by one or
more living species. Marine habitats can be divided into coastal and
open ocean habitats.

Coastal habitats are found in the area that extends from as far as the
tide comes in on the shoreline out to the edge of the continental shelf.
Most marine life is found in coastal habitats, even though the shelf
area occupies only seven percent of the total ocean. Figure 1 describes
the configuration and composition of the continental shelf showing the
coastline, the continental slope, the continental rise and the ocean
cavity.

**[Oil spills]{.underline}** frequently [kill marine
mammals]{.underline} such as whales, dolphins, seals, and sea otters.
Oil coats fur of otters and seals, leaving them vulnerable to
hypothermia. [Even when marine mammals escape the immediate effects, an
oil spill can **contaminate** their **food supply.**]{.underline}

2.2. Beaches, Marshlands, and Fragile Aquatic Ecosystems

[Oil spills]{.underline} coat everything they touch and become unwelcome
with long-term effects on parts of every ecosystem they have contact
with.﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿ When an oil slick from a large spill reaches a beach, oil coats
and clings to every rock and grain of sand.﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿ If the oil washes into
coastal marshes, mangrove forests, or other wetlands, fibrous plants and
grasses absorb oil, which can [damage plants and make the area
unsuitable as wildlife habitat]{.underline} (Fukuyama et al., 2014).

[When oil]{.underline} eventually stops floating on the water's surface
and [begins to sink into the marine environment, it can have similar
damaging effects]{.underline} on fragile

underwater ecosystems, [killing or contaminating fish and smaller
organisms that are **essential links**]{.underline} [in the]{.underline}
**[global food chain]{.underline}**.﻿﻿﻿

Despite massive clean-up efforts following the 1989 Exxon Valdez oil
spill, for example, a study conducted by the National Oceanic and
Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) found that 26,000 gallons of oil were
still trapped in the sand along the Alaska shoreline.﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿ Confirming the
recalcitrant nature of crude oil impact (Shigenaka, 2014).

2.3. Birds

Oil-covered birds are a universal symbol of environmental damage wreaked
by oil spills. Some species of shore birds might escape by relocating if
they sense danger in time, some may not. Some sea birds that swim and
dive for their food are most likely to be covered in oil following a
spill are likely vitims. Oil spills also damage nesting grounds,
potentially causing serious long-term effects on entire species.﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿ The
2010 BP Deepwater Horizon offshore oil spill in the Gulf of Mexico, for
example, occurred during prime mating and nesting season for many birds
and marine species, and long-term environmental consequences of that
spill won't be known for years.﻿﻿﻿﻿ **[Oil spills]{.underline}** [can
disrupt **migratory patterns** by contaminating areas where migrating
birds normally stop]{.underline} (Gulf Oil Spill).

[Even a small amount of oil can be deadly]{.underline} to birds.﻿﻿﻿﻿ By
coating feathers, [oil not only makes flying impossible but also
destroys birds' natural waterproofing and insulation]{.underline},
leaving them vulnerable to hypothermia or overheating.﻿﻿﻿ As birds
frantically preen their feathers to restore their natural protections,
they often swallow oil, which can severely damage their internal organs
and lead to death.﻿﻿﻿ The best estimate of the Exxon Valdez oil spill is
that it killed 250,000 seabirds (Carson et al., 1992; Haney et al.,
2014).﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿ Figures 2(a)-(c) clearly shows the degree of impacts on birds
after being oiled during oil spill incident.

The laughing gull was by far the most affected, with 32% of the entire
northern Gulf of Mexico population killed because of the spill.

An oiled seabird was found dead on the beach following the Kuroshima oil
spill near Dutch Harbor, Alaska, in November 1997 (NOAA).

2.4. Marine Mammals

[Oil spills]{.underline} frequently [kill marine mammals such as whales,
dolphins, seals, and sea otters]{.underline}.﻿﻿﻿﻿ Oil can clog blowholes of
whales and dolphins, making it impossible for them to breathe properly
and disrupting their ability to communicate. Oil coats fur of otters and
seals, leaving them vulnerable to hypothermia.

Even when marine mammals escape the immediate effects, [an oil spill can
contaminate their **food supply**]{.underline}. Marine [mammals that eat
fish or other food exposed to an oil spill may be poisoned by oil and
die or experience other problems]{.underline}.

The Exxon Valdez oil spill killed 2800 sea otters, 300 harbor seals, and
up to

22 killer whales (Harwell & Gentile, 2014). In the years after the Exxon
Valdez spill, scientists noted higher death rates among sea otters and
other species affected by the spill and stunted growth or other damage
among additional species.﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿ Thirty-five years after the disaster,
researchers have found that the Prince William Sound ecosystem seems to
have finally recovered, and localized effects on sea otters appear to
have been resolved (﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿Fukuyama et al., 2014).

[Long after the spill, **impacts persist**]{.underline} on marine
mammals leading to more dead, threat of species extinction or stunted
growth or other damage among other species as shown in Figure 3(a)
and Figure 3(b). Potential respiratory damage, hypothermia and other
cellular injuries are eminent as a result of the oil spill impact.

3\. Turtle

The survival rates of Turtles plummeted and the number of nests declined
by 35% as a result of the BP oil spill which cause a surge of sea turtle
strandings in the northern Gulf of Mexico with a majority in Alabama,
Mississippi, and Louisiana (Gallaway et al., 2016).

3.1. Impact on Fish

[Oil spills often take a deadly toll]{.underline} on fish, shellfish,
and other marine life, particularly if many fish eggs or larvae are
exposed to oil.﻿﻿﻿ Shrimp and oyster **[fisheries]{.underline}** along the
Louisiana coast [were among early **casualties**]{.underline} of the BP
Deepwater

Horizon oil spill.﻿﻿﻿﻿ Similarly, the Exxon Valdez spill destroyed billions
of salmon and herring eggs.﻿﻿﻿﻿ **[Fisheries]{.underline}** impacted by the
Exxon Valdez [took over three decades to
recover]{.underline} (Quintana-Rizzo et al., 2015).

The shrimp and oyster fisheries are usually the first casualties of
marine oil spills. In most cases, the **[fisheries hardly
recover]{.underline}**. [Fish are affected through contacts]{.underline}
with the gills, [by ingestion, or by eating oiled prey]{.underline}. The
oil contains polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs) which are the most
toxic components of oil. Oil vapors can cause damage to an organism's
central nervous system, liver, and lungs. **[Spilled oil
can]{.underline}** also have long-term reproductive problems in
organisms that have been exposed to oil (Fukuyama et al., 2014). Fishes
are very susceptible to oil spill impacts as shown in Figure 4. The
severity most often **[result]{.underline}** [to]{.underline} death,
deformation and **[extinction]{.underline}** of species.

3.2. Cetaceans

A deadly toll on dolphin and whale populations contributed to the
largest and longest marine mammal mortality event ever recorded in the
area. Between 2010 and 2014, there were 1141 cetacean strandings
recorded in the northern Gulf of Mexico, with 95% found dead. Bottlenose
dolphins especially were killed both as a direct result of oil pollution
and from long-term adverse health effects. Studies on the species
conducted from 2010 to 2015 found that reproductive success rates for
bottlenose dolphin females were less than a third of those in areas not
impacted by the spill (Kellar et al., 2017).

3.3. Wildlife Habitat and Breeding Grounds

Spill have both acute, short-term impacts on wildlife and environmental
health, and long-term effects that persist for a longer period (Schwing
et al., 2015).

[Long-term damage to species and their habitats]{.underline} and nesting
or breeding grounds [is one of the most **far-reaching environmental
impacts**]{.underline} [caused by oil spills]{.underline}.﻿﻿﻿﻿ Even species
that spend most of their lives at sea, such as various species of sea
turtles, must come ashore to nest.﻿﻿﻿﻿ Sea turtles can be harmed by oil they
encounter in the water or on the beach where they lay their eggs, their
eggs can be damaged by oil and fail to develop properly, and newly
hatched turtles may be oiled as they scurry toward the ocean across an
oily beach.﻿﻿﻿﻿﻿

### Impact\-\--Oil Spills\-\--Plankton\-\--2NC

#### Plankton collapse threatens the entire Arctic food web and accelerates climate change---oil drilling, pollution, and climate disruptions undermine core ecological and carbon cycles.

**Isibor '24** (Patrick Omoregie Isibor, Researcher in the Department of
Agriculture, specialist inPollution Studies and Ecotoxicology and a
Lecturer/Researcher in the Department of Biological Sciences at Covenant
University. He received his Ph.D. and M.Sc. in Pollution Studies &
Ecotoxicology (Environmental Quality Management) from the University of
Benin and a B.Sc. in Zoology from Ambrose Alli University. Dr. Isibor's
research interests include ecotoxicology, hydrobiology, bioaccumula-
tion, biosequestration, biodiversity conservation, and aquatic ecology.
He is a member of the Association for Environmental Impact Assessment of
Nigeria (AEIAN), the International Association of Risk and Compliance
Professionals (IARCP), and the African Society for Toxicological
Sciences (ASTS). Dr. Isibor is currently an Editor for the African
Journal of Health, Safety, and Environment, and a reviewer for several
reputable international journals. Date published: December 5 2025,
"Arctic Marine Ecotoxicology", Date access 7/1/2025)//GOATKUNA

[**Phytoplankton and zooplankton are fundamental** to the functioning of
marine ecosystems, particularly in the Arctic, where they drive food web
dynamics and biogeochemical cycles]{.underline}. Their roles in primary
production, carbon sequestration, and nutrient cycling are critical for
the health and sustainability of Arctic marine environments.
[Understanding and protecting these vital components of the ecosys- tem
are essential for managing the impacts of climate change and preserving
the biodiversity and ecological integrity of the Arctic.]{.underline}

[Human activities are increasingly **impacting the Arctic marine
environment**, with significant consequences for plankton communities.
One of the most direct impacts arises from industrial activities such as
oil and gas exploration and extraction]{.underline} \[31, 48\]. These
operations introduce [pollutants, including hydrocarbons and heavy met-
als, into the marine ecosystem, which can be **toxic to planktonic
organisms**. Oil spills, in particular, pose severe threats to
phytoplankton and zooplankton by creat- ing physical barriers to light
penetration, disrupting photosynthesis, and causing mortality through
chemical toxicity.]{.underline}

[Commercial shipping, which is expanding due to the reduction in sea
ice, also directly affects plankton communities]{.underline} \[42\].
Ships release ballast water containing non-native plankton species,
potentially [leading to **biological invasions** that disrupt local
ecosystems]{.underline}. Moreover, the discharge of waste and emissions
from ships intro- duces pollutants and alters the chemical composition
of seawater, affecting the 337 growth and survival of plankton[. Noise
pollution from industrial activities, ship- ping, and icebreaker
operations can **disrupt the behavior of zooplankton**, which rely on
acoustic signals for navigation and predator avoidance]{.underline}
\[52\]. Such disturbances can lead to changes in zooplankton
distribution and abundance, indirectly affecting the entire marine food
web.

[Climate change, driven by anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions, is
**the most significant indirect impact** on Arctic plankton
communitie]{.underline}s \[46\]. Rising tempera- tures and the melting
of sea ice are altering the physical and chemical properties of the
Arctic Ocean, with profound effects on plankton dynamics. The reduction
in sea ice extent and thickness increases light penetration into the
water column, poten- tially enhancing phytoplankton productivity.
[However, this also leads to shifts in species composition and the
timing of phytoplankton blooms, which can **disrupt the
synchronization** between phytoplankton availability and the life cycles
of zooplank- ton and higher trophic levels.]{.underline} Ocean
acidification, another consequence of increased CO2 emissions, poses a
critical threat to plankton communities. [The uptake of CO2 by seawater
reduces pH levels, affecting the calcification processes of organisms
such as coccolithophores and some species of zooplankton \[27\].
Acidification can impair their ability to build and maintain calcium
carbonate shells, leading to decreased survival and altered population
dynamics \[35\].]{.underline}

[Changes in freshwater input from melting glaciers and increased
precipitation affect the salinity and stratification of Arctic waters
\[50\]. These hydrological changes influence nutrient availability and
distribution, which are crucial for phyto- plankton growth]{.underline}.
Variations in salinity and nutrient fluxes can lead to shifts in phy-
toplankton species composition and productivity, with cascading effects
on the entire marine food web. Furthermore[, anthropogenic nutrient
inputs from riverine discharge and atmospheric deposition can cause
eutrophication, leading to **harmful algal blooms**]{.underline} (HABs)
\[60\]. [These blooms can produce toxins that are **detrimental to
marine life,** including zooplankton, and can create hypoxic conditions
that further stress plankton communities.]{.underline}

[The cumulative and synergistic effects of these direct and indirect
human activi- ties **exacerbate the stress** on Arctic plankton
communities.]{.underline} For instance, the combi- nation of warming
temperatures, altered salinity, and increased pollutant loads can create
inhospitable conditions for many plankton species. [The disruption of
plank- ton dynamics has **far-reaching implications** for the entire
Arctic marine ecosystem, as plankton form the foundation of the food
web, supporting a diverse array of marine organisms from small fish to
large marine mammals.]{.underline}

[Human activities are profoundly impacting Arctic plankton communities
through both direct mechanisms, such as pollution and noise, and
indirect pathways, such as climate change and ocean
acidification]{.underline} \[33, 39\]. The resilience and adaptability
of these communities are being tested by the rapid and multifaceted
changes occurring in the Arctic marine environment. Addressing these
challenges requires comprehen- sive and integrative management
strategies that mitigate anthropogenic pressures and enhance the
resilience of Arctic ecosystems.

### Impact\-\--Oil Spills\-\--Public Health\-\--2NC

#### Oil and gas extraction causes severe public health crises---linked to birth defects, cancer, respiratory illness, and toxic exposure

**Isibor '24** (Patrick Omoregie Isibor, Researcher in the Department of
Agriculture, specialist inPollution Studies and Ecotoxicology and a
Lecturer/Researcher in the Department of Biological Sciences at Covenant

tion, biosequestration, biodiversity conservation, and aquatic ecology.
He is a member of the Association for Environmental Impact Assessment of

Professionals (IARCP), and the African Society for Toxicological
Sciences (ASTS). Dr. Isibor is currently an Editor for the African
Journal of Health, Safety, and Environment, and a reviewer for several
reputable international journals. Date published: December 5 2025,
"Arctic Marine Ecotoxicology", Date access 7/1/2025)//GOATKUNA

According to available research, [pollution from the oil extraction
sector is probably to blame for a variety of newly discovered health
issues, such as a rise in the number of **miscarriages, eye infections,
blindness, and skin conditions**, as well as an increase in the rate of
female sterility]{.underline} \[5, 41, 45\]. In addition, a few
interviewees stated feel- ing fatigued, cramps in the stomach and
appendicitis. People inhabiting [production oil fields experience
discomfort and anguish due to noxious odors and smoke. Health
professionals in Koch and Melut have found evidence of a **link between
elevated pollution levels from the oil industry and these health
problems**]{.underline} \[29\]. [The **percentage** of mothers in the
oil-producing regions who gave birth to children with birth
abnormalities was **88.5%**, according to a research by Mckenzie et
al]{.underline}. \[33\]. [Studies conducted in the US State of Colorado
show a connection between congeni- tal heart problems and neural tube
malformations in neonates, as well as proximity to oil and gas
operations]{.underline} \[24, 33, 49\]. Piping leaks are visible in the
Mala oilfield. [Exposure to carcinogenic petrochemicals has increased in
oil-producing regions due to a typical occurrence of oil spills from
leaking pipelines, refineries, corroded or aged infrastructure, and
conflict damage]{.underline}. Another major environmental problem is the
emissions from gas flaring. [The produced gas is flared or reinjected
together with the oil throughout the mining operation. The **pollutants
resulting from incom- plete combustion are causing gas flaring,**
despite the fact that this is prohibited by law]{.underline}. Numerous
particles and other hazardous gasses are released into the atmo- sphere
in oil field environments. [Benzene, styrene, ethynyl benzene,
ethynyl-methyl benzenes, toluene, xylenes, acenaphthylene, biphenyl, and
fluorine are a few of these dangerous hydrocarbons. Some of the
recognized carcinogens in the oil leak in the Tharjat oil field are
believed to cause cancer, while others may exacerbate fetal
difficulties]{.underline}. The volunteers from Koch, Leer, and Nyal had
high levels of lead and barium in their hair, indicating an elevated
exposure to this toxic metal. [The discovery of high levels of lead in
local well water samples implies that polluted water is a major
contributing factor to the lead hair results]{.underline} \[29\].

Six typical outdoor contaminants include acid rain precursor gases (NO 2
and SO2) in addition to acidic precipitation and its detrimental effects
\[31, 32\]. [The 205 inhalation of fine particles resulting from acid
precursor gases has been **associated with heart and lung
problems**,]{.underline} including asthma and bronchitis, as well as
[early mortality]{.underline} \[4, 36\]. In addition to being a
significant contributor to the creation of the photochemical pollutant
ozone, nitrogen dioxide (NO 2) also presents a risk to human health.
Prior research has demonstrated that [animals exposed to NO2 have
**weakened defenses against bacterial and viral
infections**]{.underline}, [while youngsters exposed to high indoor NO2
concentrations may be more vulnerable to serious infections of the
lungs]{.underline}, bronchial tubes, and lower respiratory tract, as
well as bronchitis and chest cough with phlegm. Although sulfur dioxide
(SO2) is a transient irritant, [stud- ies have indicated that elevated
SO 2 levels in combination with particle matter may cause slight but
detectable transient impairments in pulmonary function]{.underline}.
[Skin lesions and malignancies may also be connected to acid
rain]{.underline}. Due to the fact that South Sudanese people mostly
rely on rainwater for drinking, cooking, laundry, and other household
purposes, drinking acidic water can change the pH of the stomach and
dissolve the mucous membrane lining the intestinal walls, increasing the
risk of stomach ulcers \[29\].

[Oil and petroleum products have a **high degree of mobility**, which
causes exten- sive environmental pollution.]{.underline} [The devastated
land area grows by **10,000 hectares**]{.underline} annually. Some
analysts claim that for every 100 km of the pipeline's path, 500
hectares of damaged land were recorded simply during the main pipeline's
con- struction. However, compared to more southern places, [the Arctic
is restoring its native plant communities at a much slower rate, and
attempts to rehabilitate con- taminated soil through technology have
proven fruitless.]{.underline} Furthermore, in actuality, reclamation
only occurs beside already-existing roadways; areas outside of these
segments remain unaffected. Frequently, there is only a phony
reclamation taking place: [the oil-filled areas are sprayed with sand,
but the oil stays in the soil and seeps into freshwater and groundwater
sources,]{.underline} eventually entering the Arctic Ocean. Every year,
hundreds of thousands of tons of petroleum products are only delivered
into the Arctic Ocean by river runoff. Because of this, the amount of
pollutants in various regions of the White, Kara, Laptev, and Barents
Seas is already two to three times higher than usual \[7\]. On Russia's
Arctic shelf, liquid hydrocarbon resources are still being developed. It
is anticipated that the Prirazlomnoye field would pro- duce five million
tons of oil by the year 2025.

### Impact\-\--Oil Spills\-\--AT: Cleanup Solves

#### Existing methods are [significantly]{.underline} slower in the Arctic.

**Park 23** \[Jiwoon Park; PhD student in chemical oceanography at
University of Washington; 8-8-2023; Impacts of oil spills in the Arctic
can last for decades; oceanbites;
https://oceanbites.org/impacts-of-oil-spills-in-the-arctic-can-last-for-decades/;
Accessed 7-1-2025\] HS 宋

[Oil has been the primary source of energy for the past century. As we
continuously search for untapped oil reserves to fuel our economy, many
have been turning their attention to the Arctic]{.underline}. The US
Geological Survey estimates that the Arctic holds up to a fifth of the
world's undiscovered oil reserves. These resources have been left
untouched, since the harsh climate conditions in the Arctic make
exploring and developing oil reserves very challenging. But oil
exploration in the Arctic is becoming more accessible with the Arctic
warming up and losing sea ice. Commercial ships that use and transport
oil are also traveling throughout the Arctic waters more often,
increasing the risk of oil spills in the Arctic. [Unfortunately, we know
**very little** about the potential impacts of oil spills in the
Arctic.]{.underline} When you think of oil spills, images of volunteers
walking along the beach and scooping out oil-drenched sands may come to
mind. These actions are definitely helpful to minimize the damage of oil
spills. [But oil in the environment also degrades naturally, just over
much longer periods of time.]{.underline} Oil-eating microbes living in
seawater and in soil can take up oil and break it down to smaller
molecules that are not harmful anymore to the environment. [However, to
break down oil effectively, these microbes need to have "nice" living
conditions (warm water temperature, lots of nutrients in water, etc.),
which the Arctic waters **don't really offer**.]{.underline} This had
motivated a group of scientists in the early 1980s to study how oil
degrades naturally in the Arctic climate, by carrying out the Baffin
Island Oil Spill (BIOS) project. In this project, they had released a
small amount of crude oil on two nearshore locations in the Arctic to
simulate real oil spill events, and tracked how the composition of oil
changed over time. Army soldiers cleaning oil at Papamoa Beach from the
Rena oil spill (Wikimedia Commons) [In 2019, another group of scientists
revisited the BIOS project sites to find out what impacts the oil spills
from four decades ago left on the beaches of Baffin Island.]{.underline}
They collected beach sand at the surface (0-2cm) and the subsurface
(slightly below the surface, 5-10cm) from the oil spill sites, and also
from uncontaminated sites. [Even though sands from the oil spill sites
did not look different from oil-free sands by eye, there was still 0-2%
and 3-6% of crude oil left in the surface and the subsurface sands after
all these years. Colwellia is a marine bacterium that can degrade oil at
cold waters (like the Arctic Ocean) (NCBI)]{.underline} The scientists
also discovered that there were still large differences in the
populations and types of microbes living in oil-contaminated sands
compared to oil-free sands. Because oil-contaminated sands were
providing extra nutrients only for oil-eating microbes, oil-contaminated
sands contained mostly oil-eating microbes, whereas oil-free sands
contained many more types of diverse microbes. [This meant that the
sands affected by oil still **haven't returned** to their original
state!]{.underline} Interestingly, most of the oil-eating microbes were
not specifically adapted to the cold temperatures in the Arctic. [This
means that oil-eating bacteria are likely breaking down oil at a **much
slower pace** than they would be if they were living in warm
waters.]{.underline} This result is important for us to think about how
to clean up oil spills in the Arctic, because we literally cannot heat
up the Arctic waters to help microbes break down oil quickly. Instead,
we may have to rely more on "aggressive" cleanup strategies, by directly
removing the beach soil and treating them somewhere else to get rid of
oil. Overall, studies like these are very important in helping us better
understand the potential danger of oil spills in the Arctic (and
possibly Antarctic), and come up with strategies to efficiently clean up
oil and recover the ecosystem.

#### The Arctic is uniquely hard to clean up.

Talal **Husseini 18**, Reporter at Offshore Technology, "Oil spills in
the ocean: why the Arctic is particularly vulnerable," Offshore
Technology, 08-14-2018, accessed 07-01-2025,
https://www.offshore-technology.com/features/oil-spills-in-the-ocean-arctic,
DG

[During]{.underline} the [clean-up]{.underline} operations
[of]{.underline} the Exxon [Valdez]{.underline} spill in Alaska [and
Deepwater Horizon]{.underline} in the Gulf of Mexico, [only 15% to 25%
of oil was successfully removed through mechanical methods]{.underline},
such as mechanical recovery and burning of the spilled oil. [The bulk of
the clean-up was carried out by oil-eating bacteria]{.underline} in the
water.

Of particular interest to scientists is the way in which [oil-eating
microbes are **slowed down in ice-cold temperatures**]{.underline}. The
Arctic Research Centre at Aarhus University in Denmark has found that
[low temperatures change the chemical properties of]{.underline} the
[spilled oil and **slow down biodegradation**. Cold oil is]{.underline}
said to be [more viscous, making it harder for]{.underline} oil-eating
[microbes to break it down]{.underline}.

[The **lack of waves** in the Arctic Ocean poses another challenge.
Where there is sea ice and]{.underline} therefore [fewer
waves]{.underline}, the [oil does not disperse into small
droplets.]{.underline}

[There is also a lower level of nutrients, such as nitrogen and
phosphorus, which feed algae and bacteria]{.underline} in the water.
[Without these]{.underline} nutrients, the [**bacteria cannot develop**
at an optimum rate]{.underline}.

Another interesting feature is the [long]{.underline} (often 24-hour)
[periods of sunlight in the Arctic summer]{.underline}, which can both
help and hinder clean-up operations. On the one hand, long hours of
sunlight help the microbes to break up oil molecules, but conversely
this could [make the oil compounds **more toxic for aquatic
organisms**]{.underline}.

#### Arctic oil spills are impossible to clean up.

Ed **Struzik 15**, Fellow at the Queen's University Institute for Energy
and Environmental Policy, Contributing Writer at Yale Environment 360,
"Oil Drilling in Arctic Ocean: A Push into Uncharted Waters," Yale
Environment 360, 06-08-2015, accessed 07-01-2025,
https://e360.yale.edu/features/oil_drilling_in_arctic_ocean_a_push_into_uncharted_waters,
DG

Last October, an unmanned barge with 950 gallons of fuel on board was in
the Beaufort Sea when it broke free from the tug towing it. The weather
was stormy and the tug captain decided it was too dangerous to try to
retrieve the barge in turbulent seas.

Ideally, the Canadian Coast Guard would have been on hand to deal with
the situation. But the icebreaker it routinely dispatches to the
Beaufort Sea had gone back to Vancouver for the winter. It would have
taken a week for it to return.

In the days that followed, powerful currents swept the barge into
Alaskan waters. The U.S. Coast Guard couldn't do anything because its
one operating icebreaker was stationed in Antarctica at the time.
Canadian and U.S. officials thought the barge would most likely be
locked in the ice, or crushed by ice and sink. It continued to drift,
however, and when satellite observers checked last month the barge was
about 40 miles off Russia's Chukchi Peninsula. Russian attempts to find
the barge have been hampered, likely to due to bad weather.

As potential oil spills go, the barge incident is an extremely minor
one. But experts say that the errant barge should be a wake-up call for
[Arctic nations]{.underline} like the United States, Canada, Russia,
Denmark, and Norway that [are poised to ramp up shipping, as well as oil
and gas drilling]{.underline}, in the Arctic.

Not only does the route of the ghost barge serve as a real-life
demonstration of how spilled oil could move across the Arctic, it also
illustrates how difficult it will be to contain and clean up oil in a
region where roads, ports, airplanes, and icebreakers are few and far
between. In addition, [scientists and engineers]{.underline} still
[**don't fully understand** how chemical dispersants and oil collection
agents might work in a cold climate, how ice conditions and ocean
currents]{.underline} will [influence the fate of spilled oil, and how
well microbes in the Arctic]{.underline} are able to
[degrade]{.underline} the [hydrocarbons, as they tend to do in warmer
climates]{.underline}, such as during the 2010 Deepwater Horizon oil
spill in the Gulf of Mexico.

"[Responding to an oil spill is **extremely challenging in any marine
environment**]{.underline}," says Paul Crowley, the Canadian director of
the World Wildlife Fund's Arctic Program. "[Under Arctic conditions, it
is **much worse**. A major spill would contaminate ice and shorelines
for]{.underline} many [thousands of kilometers, kill seabirds and
mammals, and severely pollute the natural ecosystem that]{.underline}
traditional \[[indigenous]{.underline}\] [users and wildlife rely
upon]{.underline}."

Crowley noted that the offshore operating season in the Arctic --- and
therefore [the period when it would be possible to clean up an oil
spill]{.underline} --- [is restricted to four to five months by
darkness, heavy ice, and extreme cold]{.underline}. These [severe
conditions]{.underline} would [make it **impossible** to attempt an oil
spill cleanup for half of the time during]{.underline} the [operating
season, and **100 percent of the time during the winter**]{.underline},
Crowley and others say.

The issue of oil spill response in the Arctic has gained added attention
since the Obama administration announced last month that it was giving
conditional approval to Shell Oil to drill up to six exploratory wells
in the Chukchi Sea off Alaska. Although the U.S. is requiring Shell to
abide by stringent safeguards --- drilling only in shallow water and
only in ice-free months, requiring the presence of a second oil rig to
drill a relief well in case of a blowout --- the administration's
decision nonetheless opens the door to drilling in U.S. Arctic waters.

Shell also is exploring Arctic oil-drilling opportunities in Russia,
Greenland, and Norway. The company says it is taking "extraordinary
precautions" to drill safely and is collaborating closely with
scientific organizations and governments in the region.

Using a supposedly ice-resistant platform, Russia has already begun
drilling in the Arctic Ocean at the Prirazlomnoye oil field in the
Pechora Sea off western Siberia. These developments, experts warn, may
be just the beginning of oil exploitation in a region that is unprepared
to handle oil spills of any size.

Rob Huebert is senior research fellow at the Center for Military and
Strategic Studies at the University of Calgary and a board member of the
Canadian Polar Commission, which is responsible for monitoring and
disseminating knowledge of the polar regions. He is not alone in
suggesting that the time to act on oil spill preparedness should come
sooner rather than later because oil and gas drilling, as well as
shipping in the Arctic, will inevitably accelerate.

In addition to Russia, Huebert says, other Arctic nations --- including
Greenland, Norway, the United States, and Canada --- are interested in
developing energy resources in the Arctic, setting "these states on a
collision course with the international environmental community."

Recent oil spills such as Deepwater Horizon have provided engineers and
biologists with valuable insights into the containment, cleanup, and
disposal of spilled oil. These spills have also spurred the U.S.
Department of Interior to enact some of the most aggressive reforms to
offshore oil and gas regulation and oversight in U.S. history.

But [as Deepwater Horizon demonstrated, it takes a **tremendous amount
of resources** to clean up a major spill]{.underline}. At the height of
the cleanup, 7,000 vessels, 125 aircraft, and 47,000 people were
deployed from a host of easily accessible ports and bases across the
coast of the Gulf of Mexico. [And yet, several months after the oil well
was finally capped, more than 100 millions gallons of oil]{.underline}
--- out of as many as 210 million spilled --- [was still unaccounted
for]{.underline}. Much of that [missing oil]{.underline} is believed to
have [settled on the sea floor]{.underline}.

A spill on the scale of the Deepwater Horizon isn't going to happen
anytime soon in the Arctic. But experts say that mo[bilizing even a
fraction of these resources in the region in an expeditious manner would
be **extremely difficult** because there are **few roads, runways, or
ports**]{.underline} in the Arctic; **[little expert manpower standing
by]{.underline}** to stage a response; [and **insufficient international
protocols** to orchestrate a cleanup if]{.underline} the [oil crosses
international boundaries. Tracking]{.underline} the oil [by satellite
would]{.underline} also [be difficult because of weather conditions and
ice that may cover oil slicks]{.underline}.

Studies have shown that in most cases, no more than 20 per cent of oil
spilled in the ocean can be recovered by mechanical means --- those that
do not involve the use of chemicals, which can be toxic to marine
organisms. In situ burning and chemical dispersants, therefore, would
have to be relied upon heavily in the event of a spill in the Arctic,
experts say.

University of Manitoba scientist David Barber heads up a Canadian
research group with expertise in the detection, impacts, and mitigation
of oil in sea ice. He says [there is very limited knowledge of how
Arctic marine ecosystems will be affected by]{.underline} the presence,
composition, and dispersion of [oil, as well as chemicals used for
cleanup]{.underline}, such as dispersants.

"Development of [technologies]{.underline} that would be able to help
detect oil in ice, and cold-adapted bioremediation technologies, [are
**in their infancy**]{.underline}," he points out.

Barber and his group recently received \$28 million in funding from the
Canadian government to study ways of detecting oil in Arctic ice. He
says there are a number of variables in the Arctic that complicate
containment and clean up efforts, with ice and cold weather topping the
list. [Oil can become frozen in ice and drift long
distances]{.underline}, and [oil trapped under ice]{.underline} also
[breaks down more slowly because of reduced wave action. Lower
evaporation losses in cold climes]{.underline} also [slow the breakdown
of oil]{.underline}. The [long-lingering effects of the
1989]{.underline} Exxon [Valdez oil spill in southern Alaska
demonstrated the difficulties]{.underline} of cleaning up oil spills in
cold climes.

What worries scientists and environmentalists even more is [a
blowout]{.underline} that [might take place toward the end of the summer
drilling season]{.underline}.

"If a blowout were to occur and could not be contained before the winter
freeze-up, [it could **spew oil uncontrollably for the seven or eight
months** of winter ice-cover, without the **possibility of** taking any
**steps during that time to control it**]{.underline}," Crowley says.
"[The oil would bind with the newly formed ice, be carried far and wide
by ocean currents, and **released into new environments** the following
spring]{.underline}." This, Barber said, should cause people "to pause
and reflect on the consequences of allowing oil and gas development in
this region without the proper safeguards."

Environmental groups are particularly concerned that Shell is being
given permits for conditional drilling in the Arctic, considering that
the company's history of exploratory drilling has been fraught with
problems, including the grounding of a drilling rig off the Alaska coast
in late 2012.

Shell has made improvements in source control capping and containment
since 2012, but experts with WWF and the Pew Charitable Trusts believe
that Shell's spill response capabilities, and those of U.S. agencies,
still do not come close to resembling what an expert committee for the
U.S. National Research Council recommended in 2014. The report
highlighted serious shortcomings and featured a long list of
recommendations.

"**[There is still no backstop]{.underline}** --- [no]{.underline}
national [regulation to ensure operators are prepared for the harsh
conditions and remote location of the Arctic]{.underline} region," says
Marilyn Heiman, director of Pew's US Arctic Project. "That's why we are
pushing for \[the\] Interior \[Department\] to finalize strong
regulations that will apply to all future drilling in the Arctic."

The fact that Russia is ramping up activity in the Arctic was one of the
concerns of the National Research Council committee. If a spill or an
accident were to happen on the Russian side of the border, the committee
pointed out, oil could easily migrate into Alaskan waters. The committee
called on the U.S. Coast Guard to expand its bilateral agreement with
Russia to include Arctic spill scenarios. Crowley says the same
international dispersion of oil could happen if a spill were to occur in
Canada's Beaufort Sea, where Imperial Oil Canada, Exxon Mobil, and BP
have jointly filed an application to drill at least one well nearly a
mile deep.

Crowley says that the barge that ended up in Russia is an example of
what WWF-Canada has highlighted in an oil spill modeling map of the
Beaufort Sea. The barge has followed roughly the same path along the
Canadian, Alaskan, and Russian coasts as many of the 22 oil spill
scenarios depicted on the WWF map.

"This also highlights the need for increased cooperation, both between
industry and governments, and especially between the Arctic states who
share these waters," says Crowley.

### Impact\-\--Permafrost\-\--1NC

#### Rapid permafrost melt blows up the entire atmosphere

**Wollersheim 25** \[Dominic Wollersheim; Job One for Humanity Executive
Director; 6-18-2025; \"sixth mass extinction event at Job One for
Humanity\"; Job One for Humanity;
https://www.joboneforhumanity.org/tags/sixth_mass_extinction_event;
Accessed 6-27-2025; AY\]

[A **total extinction** event could begin]{.underline} as early as 50-70
years from now[, in the form of a very high average global temperature
and very high atmospheric carbon levels]{.underline} of approximately
750 ppm, which would support the initiation of the final stages of the
runaway global heating process.

[The final stage, runaway global heating, describes the circumstances in
which the global climate destabilizes **catastrophically and
permanently** from its original state---similar to what happened on
Venus when the planet lost its atmosphere out into outer
space.]{.underline} Runaway global warming is thought to have occurred
on Venus approximately 4 billion years ago due to its extremely high
carbon-rich atmosphere and exceptionally high average surface
temperatures.

[The final stage of runaway global warming will create a literal
Climageddon meltdown where nothing will survive because there will be
**no atmosphere**.]{.underline} This total extinction event will enter
its first phase as soon as our average global temperature rises at or
above 5- 6 degrees Celsius.

Somewhere at a 5-6 degree Celsius increase in average global
temperature, [added to the massive additional tipping point of
**releases of methane** from coastal deposits and
permafrost]{.underline}, will quickly [further skyrocket atmospheric
carbon levels and average global temperatures]{.underline} to new levels
faster than at any previous time in humanity\'s history. This will also
trigger, initiate, and accelerate the final phases of runaway global
warming.

Here is how this happens. At a 5-6 degree Celsius temperature increase,
[the coastal deposits and permafrost begin releasing methane at
**exponentially higher rates**, or in other words, the \"methane time
bomb\" goes off.]{.underline}

This will take us from the 5-6 degree Celsius atmospheric carbon level
of 500-600 parts per million (ppm) far too quickly to the atmospheric
carbon levels of 800 ppm, 1,000 ppm, 1,200 ppm, and even to 1,600 ppm
and beyond. (Our climate was stable for hundreds of thousands of years
at about carbon 270 ppm.) We are currently at about carbon 420 ppm. (The
recent carbon 270 to 415 ppm increase occurred since the beginning of
the Industrial Revolution, which was powered by burning fossil fuels.)

### Impact\-\--Permafrost\-\--2NC

#### Melting permafrost is a [ticking time bomb]{.underline}

**Do and Rutka 23** \[Liz Do, science communicator with more than 10
years of experience, having previously worked at the University of
Toronto Engineering as its chief writer. She holds a degree in
Journalism from Toronto Metropolitan University; & Alison Rutka,
award-winning communications professional, spent the last 12 years
working at both for-profit and not-for-profit organizations. Most
recently, she led the rebrand of the National Payroll Institute and
built strategic campaigns for both B2B and B2C audiences, Alison has a
BA Honours from Western University, an MA in Literatures of Modernity
from Toronto Metropolitan University, and several professional
certificates from the University of Toronto and York University.
\"Unearthing the "carbon bomb" hiding underground\", 24 Oct, 2023,
CIFAR, https://cifar.ca/unearthing-the-carbon-bomb-hiding-underground/,
accessed 7-1-2025\] /vzhu

[Jacqueline Goordial, a CIFAR Azrieli **Global Scholar**]{.underline} in
the Earth 4D: Subsurface Science & Exploration program, recalls her last
visit to the Canadian Arctic. From the helicopter, she and her team flew
over a [large **permafrost** site]{.underline} that is [thawing at such
a pace that the edges of its structure are slumping]{.underline}, as
though a giant had taken a bite out of the soil underneath the surface.

Once on land, and venturing through thigh-high mud and tundra, the team
drove their instruments deep into the permafrost face, next to a
**[thousand year old]{.underline}** wedge of ice formation --- they were
there to [measure **methane** levels seeping out from the frozen
ground.]{.underline}

The [next day,]{.underline} they came back to find the [permafrost thaw
face had **melted away**]{.underline}, along with their instruments.

"That was [less than **24 hours**]{.underline}," says Goordial. "This is
a [structure]{.underline} that's been [frozen for thousands of
years]{.underline}, and I [see it erode in real time, inch by inch,
within a day]{.underline}."

The rapid erosion of permafrost is one of a myriad of alarming examples
of the effects of climate change --- whether it is shrinking glaciers,
frequent extreme weather events, increased food scarcity or rising sea
levels. Goordial's work examines how [**microbial life persists** within
extreme subsurface environments such as permafrost]{.underline} and
oceanic crust/marine sediments.

Though Goordial's instruments were irretrievable in that instance, her
team has been successful in collecting methane data in the Canadian
Arctic.

"We [install chambers into the face of the permafrost]{.underline} and
one of the things that we [see]{.underline} is that there is an
[**instant release** of methane coming from these **fast erosional**
features]{.underline}," explains Goordial. "We have [a lot of **old
carbon** frozen away.]{.underline}"

[As the permafrost is rapidly melting, it is releasing trapped methane
from **microorganisms** underground. Methane is **25 times** as potent
at trapping heat in the atmosphere as carbon dioxide (CO2), and
eventually **combines** with oxygen to create **CO2**.]{.underline}

[Permafrost in the Northern Hemisphere is currently trapping about
**1,700 tonnes of carbon**, including methane and CO2. In]{.underline} a
March 2021 feature in [Nature]{.underline} on effects of thawing
permafrost, [climate scientists describe the disappearing permafrost
landscape as having the potential to trigger a **massive "carbon
bomb."**]{.underline}

"One of the things that we fear will happen when it thaws is [some of
that carbon will become **newly available** to microorganisms that have
gone from this frozen state, to a **warmer state**]{.underline}. Then,
[through their own **metabolism**, these microorganisms will convert
that carbon into **more greenhouse gases**]{.underline}," explains
Goordial.

#### It turns infrastructure and triggers positive feedback loops. 

**Isibor 24** (Patrick Omoregie Isibor, Researcher in the Department of
Agriculture, specialist in Pollution Studies and Ecotoxicology and a
Lecturer/Researcher in the Department of Biological Sciences at Covenant
University. He received his Ph.D. and M.Sc. in Pollution Studies &
Ecotoxicology (Environmental Quality Management) from the University of
Benin and a B.Sc. in Zoology from Ambrose Alli University. Dr. Isibor's
research interests include ecotoxicology, hydrobiology, bioaccumulation,
bio sequestration, biodiversity conservation, and aquatic ecology. He is
a member of the Association for Environmental Impact Assessment of
Nigeria (AEIAN), the International Association of Risk and Compliance

Sciences (ASTS). Dr. Isibor is currently an Editor for the African
Journal of Health, Safety, and Environment, and a reviewer for several
reputable international journals. Date published: December 5, 2025,
"Arctic Marine Ecotoxicology", Date access 7/1/2025)//daxw

Additionally, [permafrost temperatures are rising, leading to thawing
that releases stored greenhouse gases]{.underline}, further amplifying
global warming \[94\]. The [thawing of permafrost in the Arctic has
far-reaching consequences for both infrastructure and
ecosystems]{.underline}. Permafrost, which is ground that remains frozen
for at least two consecutive years, underpins much of the Arctic
landscape. As temperatures rise, the stability of this frozen ground is
compromised, [leading to]{.underline} several negative impacts such as
[structural damage in buildings, roads, pipelines, and other
infrastructure built on permafrost which are at risk of damage as the
ground thaws]{.underline} and becomes J. O. Kayode-Edwards et al. 79
unstable \[84\]. The thawing of permafrost can also affect the
reliability of transportation networks as roads and airstrips can become
uneven or impassable, disrupting travel and supply routes for Arctic
communities \[25\].

Furthermore, [settlement]{.underline} (the compression of the ground
beneath a building due to its weight) and subsistence (the movement of
foundations due to the action of materials beneath them) [can cause
foundations to crack and collapse, necessitating costly repairs and
redesigns]{.underline}. [Changes in vegetation and ground stability
impact wildlife habitats]{.underline}. Species that rely on specific
conditions, such as the tundra-dwelling caribou and migratory birds, may
find their habitats shrinking or shifting. The thawing permafrost alters
the landscape, affecting plant communities as well. As the ground
becomes wetter, some areas may experience increased plant growth, while
others may see a decline in species that depend on dry, frozen
conditions. The permafrost thawing equally affects the hydrological
cycle, leading to changes in the flow of rivers and streams \[97\].

This can impact fish populations and other aquatic life, as well as
water availability for human use. [Faster flow rates and increased
sediment load can affect fish spawning areas and reduce water quality,
potentially leading to shifts in species composition and declines in
biodiversity]{.underline}.

Permafrost contains vast amounts of organic material that, when frozen,
is relatively stable. However, [as permafrost thaws, this organic
material decomposes, releasing greenhouse gases into the atmosphere.
Methane is a particularly potent greenhouse gas, with a global warming
potential many times greater than that of carbon dioxide over a 20-year
period]{.underline} \[28\]. [The release of methane from thawing
permafrost can signifcantly amplify global warming as it creates a
positive feedback loop]{.underline}. As more methane is released, global
temperatures rise further, leading to more permafrost thaw and
additional greenhouse gas emissions.

Thawing permafrost also leads to the release of CO2 through the
increased decomposition of organic material. This contributes to the
overall greenhouse gas burden in the atmosphere, with potential impacts
on weather patterns, sea level rise, and the frequency of extreme
weather events. [Current climate models may underestimate the potential
contributions of permafrost thaw to future greenhouse gas
concentrations]{.underline}, highlighting the need for more
comprehensive data and improved modeling.

#### Feedback loops make [any]{.underline} climate efforts ineffective

**Flanagan 21** \[Erin Flanagan, geopolitical analyst, researcher and
writer with The Arctic Institute, specializing in environmental policy
and Arctic climate dynamics. \"The Global Carbon Budget and Permafrost
Feedback Loops in the Arctic\", February 25, 2021, Arctic Institute -
Center for Circumpolar Security Studies,
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/global-carbon-budget-permafrost-feedback-loops-arctic/,
accessed 7-3-2025\] /vzhu

[**Permafrost thaw** is one of the world's **most pressing** climate
problems,]{.underline} already disrupting lifestyles, livelihoods,
economies, and ecosystems in the north, and [threatening to spill beyond
the boundaries of the Arctic as our planet continues to
warm]{.underline}. To examine the effects of permafrost degradation, and
increase our understanding of what this phenomenon means for the future
of the region (and the world), The Arctic Institute's new two-part
permafrost series aims to analyze the topic from scientific, security,
legal, and personal perspectives. The second installment of our series
features eight new articles on permafrost degradation and its effects on

seven articles from our first installment, starting with the Intro.

Permafrost Thaw in the Warming Arctic: Final Remarks

[**Siberia**, like much of the world, had a year like no
other]{.underline}. Stories about an [**unprecedented six-month
heatwave** and raging wildfires throughout Russia's Northern
regions]{.underline} filtered into the mainstream media. These alarming
headlines reminded the world that climate change and its effects are
being felt now, especially in the Far North. The [Arctic]{.underline},
which is [warming]{.underline} at least [**two times faster** than the
rest of the world]{.underline}, is a [harbinger **signaling dire
consequences** if we cannot keep global warming below **1.5 degrees
Celsius**]{.underline}.1) Hidden underneath these extreme events is a
quieter but no less pressing [threat]{.underline} that could prevent us
from meeting that goal; one that may prove [pivotal]{.underline} if we
hope [to curb global carbon emissions: **thawing
permafrost**.]{.underline}

In order to be considered [permafrost]{.underline}, the ground must
[remain]{.underline} at or below [freezing for at least two consecutive
years]{.underline}.2) It traditionally [acts as a "**carbon sink**,"
where carbon has been captured and stored. Scientists estimate that this
layer contains approximately **1,400 gigatons** of frozen carbon, the
equivalent of **four times** the amount **humans** have emitted since
the Industrial Revolution and **two times** as much as is **currently**
**contained** in the environment.3) If this captured carbon were allowed
to escape into the atmosphere, it would contribute to]{.underline} what
may already be [a **runaway greenhouse effect** that could render even
the **most ambitious** attempts to cut global emissions **ineffective**.
Essentially, we could make **every necessary sacrifice** and still see
global temperatures rise above 1.5 degrees Celsius if the Arctic becomes
a major carbon source instead of remaining a carbon sink]{.underline}. A
recent NASA-funded study suggests that this shift is already happening
due to winter soil emissions. According to Sue Natali of the Woods Hole
Research Center, carbon emissions at individual sites are surpassing
carbon uptake. It remains to be seen if this transition is occurring
throughout the entire Arctic region. This development makes
understanding and incorporating permafrost thaw emissions into the
global carbon budget even more pressing.4)

Despite the potential ramifications of emissions from permafrost thaw,
[the **global carbon budget**, which balances the carbon dioxide (CO2)
output from human activities with the input or storage in carbon sinks,
does **not fully** **incorporate** this emission source]{.underline}.5)
Ensuring that permafrost thaw is included in this calculation is
critical in making our current goals reachable. Fortunately, the recent
events in Siberia are bringing a new level of awareness to the [leading
role]{.underline} that [permafrost will play in]{.underline} our future
[**success or failure** in staying below 1.5 degrees of
warming.]{.underline} In examining these events, we can better
understand the relationship between extreme weather, its impact on
permafrost, and the consequences if the international community fails to
account for this looming threat.

The six-month heatwave in Siberia contributed to raising the world's
average temperature to the hottest on record.6) [From January through
the end of June, Siberia was subjected to record high temperatures. One
of the world's **coldest towns,** Verkhoyansk, saw **100-degree
days**]{.underline}.7) According to Zeke Hausfather, the Director of
Climate and Energy at the Breakthrough Institute in Oakland, California,
Siberia's temperatures were "extreme no matter how you look at them."8).
These [temperatures]{.underline} concerned scientists because of their
[potential to **increase the rate of thaw** in the Arctic. High,
prolonged temperatures melt more ice and snow, which expose the **darker
sub-layers.** Without the layer of white snow and the **protective
albedo effect** it provides, the soil begins to **thaw**. This, in turn,
exposes more dark soil, which leads to further melting and so on. As the
thaw moves deeper into the soil, **nutrients are released**, allowing
plants and shrubs to grow, darkening the surface even more. As this
**positive, self-reinforcing feedback** continues, more CO2 is emitted.
Carbon dioxide is a significant contributor to global warming, resulting
in more **extreme weather** and other climate events that amplify the
cycle. Scientists fear that these local feedback loops will contribute
to a **global loop** with **disastrous, irreversible**
consequences.]{.underline} Unfortunately, high temperatures have also
led to other devastating events in the Arctic.

In Siberia, these protracted, unusually [high temperatures]{.underline}
have [result]{.underline}ed [in]{.underline} conditions that have
contributed to **[extreme wildfires]{.underline}**. According to the
National Aeronautics and Space Administration's Earth Observatory,
Siberia's 2020 fire season released more carbon dioxide in June and July
than any other fire season since 2003.9) These wildfires [**burn off
the** **organic layers** of moss and other decomposing plants that form
on top of permafrost-affected soils. That organic matter typically acts
as a **blanket** in the summertime to keep the darker, deeper soil
insulated from the heat of the sun. However, the deeper layers of
permafrost, having already thawed more than usual during the winter
months from a reduced albedo effect, are now **more susceptible to
thaw** in layers that would typically stay frozen even in the summer
months. When **wildfires burn off** that summertime insulation, the
initial loss of insulating materials kicks off a sequence of **local
feedback loops, amplifying** permafrost thaw.]{.underline}10) Dr.
Charles Koven, an Earth System Scientist for the University of
California, Berkeley, notes that the permafrost research community's
[ability to incorporate extreme events like wildfires into their climate
models is limited]{.underline}. However, [there is]{.underline} also a
[basic agreement]{.underline} that "there is a possibility that these
[extreme events]{.underline} are **[amplify]{.underline}**ing [and
speed]{.underline}ing [up things]{.underline}."

There is a [growing **consensus** among scientists and policymakers that
this critical emissions source needs to be better
represented]{.underline} in our climate models. Many in the permafrost
research community, those who have been studying it and speaking up
about it for years, worry that despite our best efforts and sacrifices,
[we may **not be able** to keep global warming below the goal of 1.5
degrees **unless we consider these** **feedback loops.**]{.underline}
Dr. Koven estimates that about 100 gigatons of CO2 will need to be
subtracted from the current global carbon budget to account for these
emissions. While there is still significant uncertainty concerning the
amount of CO2 held in permafrost, [the impact of extreme events, and the
rate of thaw]{.underline}, it [is vital that the international community
not ignore the situation]{.underline} until the models are improved.
With permafrost thaw incorporated into the global carbon budget,
policymakers will be better able to make decisions based on a more
realistic picture of the actions countries will need to take to meet
their carbon emission goals. Even so, including estimated emissions from
permafrost thaw will just be the first step. Ongoing international
cooperation will be essential in developing more effective observation
networks and funding additional research in this critical area.

**OA -- ICC \'16** \[Ocean Acidification International Coordination

#### **Radiation leakage causes extinction**

**Bonacic et Al. 23** \[Cristian Bonacic, department of Ecosystems & The

https://www.frontiersin.org/journals/ecology-and-evolution/articles/10.3389/fevo.2022.1099162/full;
